In general, ascending mountains without selecting the proper day and hour may still yield results, but such attempts should not be taken lightly.

Great and small mountains alike possess **divine spirits (神灵)**—the greater the mountain, the greater its deity; the smaller the mountain, the lesser its deity. Without proper precautions, harm will surely follow.

Some may suffer **illness (疾病)**, others **frightful shocks (惊怖)**, some **injuries from thorns or blades (伤刺)**, some **strange sounds (异声)**, some **encounter anomalies (变异)**, some **see spectral apparitions (光景)**, or witness **great trees collapse without wind (不风而摧折)** or **cliffs crumble without cause (无辜而自堕)**. Others may be driven to **madness and confusion (迷乱)**, causing them to **stumble and fall into ravines (狂走坠落坑谷)**, or **fierce beasts and venomous serpents (猛兽毒蛇)** may attack them. Hence, one must not ascend lightly.

When entering mountains, one must first choose days aligned with the **Four Seasons' Celestial Masters (四时王相)** and their **Stems and Branches (支干)** in harmonious generation. Such days are favorable.

Moreover, if one follows the **auspicious timing of the Evasion Hexagram (遁甲)**, the result is even better.

One must also select the **auspicious days within the lunar month (月中吉日)**.

If there is urgency and one cannot wait for a fully auspicious day, one should at least avoid the **taboo days of the month (束岳岁月忌)**. For instance:
- **Huàshān (華山)** forbids entry during **Years of Jia and Yi (甲乙)** or **Years of Yín and Mǎo (寅卯)**, specifically in **Months 1 and 2 (正月、二月)**.
- **Nányuè (南岳)** forbids entry during **Years of Bǐng and Dīng (丙丁)** or **Years of Jǐ and Wǔ (己午)**, specifically in **Months 4 and 5 (四月、五月)**.
- **Zhōngyuè (中岳)** forbids entry during **Years of Wù and Sì (戊巳)**, **Years of Chén and Xū (辰戌)**, or **Years of Chǒu and Wèi (丑未)**, in **all four seasons (四季之月)**.
- **Xīyuè (西岳)** forbids entry during **Years of Gēng and Xīn (庚辛)** or **Years of Shēn and Yǒu (申酉)**, specifically in **Months 7 and 8 (七月、八月)**.
- **Běiyuè (北岳)** forbids entry during **Years of Rén and Guǐ (壬癸)** or **Years of Hài and Zǐ (亥子)**, specifically in **Months 10 and 11 (十月、十一月)**.

The above restrictions do not apply only to the **Five Sacred Peaks (五岳)**. Rather, for any mountain one ascends, one must observe the taboos of its corresponding **Sacred Peak category (方面)**.

The **"Scripture of the Soul’s Treasure (《灵宝经》)"** states:
*"When ascending mountains, one must choose **Preserving Days (保日)**, **Righteous Days (义日)**, or **Exclusive Days (专日)**—these are of great auspiciousness. If one ascends on **Restraining Days (制日)** or **Slaying Days (伐日)**, death will surely follow."*

**Preserving Days (保日)** refer to days where the **Stems and Branches (支干)** generate harmoniously above and below—such as **Jia-Wǔ (甲午)** or **Yi-Sì (乙巳)**. **Jia (甲)** is Wood, **Wǔ (午)** is Fire; **Yi (乙)** is Wood, **Sì (巳)** is Fire. Since Fire is born from Wood, these combinations align with the **Evasion Hexagram’s principles (遣法)**, integrating both **talismans (符)** and **Daoist prohibitions (数道)**, making them most efficacious.

Thus, the **talismanic methods (符法)** are detailed in the later volumes. **End of Volume Four of the Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Scripture of Divine Elixir (黄帝九鼎神丹经诀卷之四竟)**.

**Volume Five of the Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Scripture of Divine Elixir (黄帝九鼎神丹经诀卷之五)**.

The **Crimson Elixir’s** attainment of the **Divine Dan** must rely on the **Dao’s** profound resources.

I have heard that to seek **long life and eternal vision**, to harmonize with Heaven and Earth—if one has not grasped the **ultimate essentials**, then the lesser arts must still be known. For they collectively fulfill the **Dao** of **nourishing life**.

In grand terms, it is like a sovereign governing a state: both **literary and martial** arts must be employed without omission. In minor terms, it is like crafting a chariot: no single component—**axle, hub, or rim**—can be neglected.

What is called the **Dao** encompasses **internal cultivation of form and spirit**, prolonging life and healing ailments, and **external warding of malevolence**, preventing calamities from encroaching. It is like a **zither or se**, where a single string cannot produce harmony, or like **armor and weapons**, where a single reinforcement cannot withstand the blade.

Those who **nourish life** must seek **broad knowledge** while valuing the **essential**, observe **diverse methods** while choosing the **virtuous**. A single practice, no matter how refined, cannot suffice alone.

Moreover, there are those who pursue **esoteric arts** but cling to their own strengths. Those who speak of **playing with the Three Pure Ones** claim only the **Chamber of Mystery** can prolong life. Those who elucidate **breathing techniques** assert only **qi cultivation** can extend years. Those who know the **laws of contraction and extension** say only **guiding and pulling** can defy aging. Those who master **herbal prescriptions** declare their remedies can cure all.

Those who fail in the **Dao** do so by relying on **partial methods alone**. Once the **Great Elixir** is perfected, all else becomes unnecessary. Before its completion, nothing can be spared.

It is like a farmer tending to his fields: if he possesses **gold, silver, jewels, and pearls** in his embrace, sufficient to sustain generations, he rests assured. But if he lacks such wealth, how can he not sow **a hundred grains** and store **abundant vegetables**?

Thus, **fasting to abstain from grains**, **warding off weapons**, **suppressing ghosts and demons**, **summoning and controlling poisons**, **healing diseases and curing illnesses**—when entering mountains, ensuring **ferocious beasts do not attack**; when crossing waters, preventing **dragon serpents from harming**; **snakes from biting**, **epidemics from striking**—these are minor matters, yet those who **nourish life** must not overlook them.

The **Divine Elixir** is the **greatest matter of longevity**; how can it be delayed?

In antiquity, the **Yellow Emperor** crossed **Ronghu Mountain** to receive the **Scripture of the Elixir**, ascended **Kongtong Peak** to question **Broad Successor**, and sought the **Dao of nourishing life**, hearing the **doctrines of the Three Pure Ones**. When exploring **mysteries and marvels**, he recorded them in the **words of Bai Ze**. Thus, exhausting the **truths of the Dao**, encompassing all **secret principles**, he **refined the Nine Transformations** beneath **Jing Mountain**, and **flowed like pearls** above **Ding Lake**. Through the **power of the Divine Elixir** and the **achievement of eternal vision**, he ultimately **ascended the Dragon’s Peak**, transcending with Heaven and Earth without limit.

After the **Dao** was perfected, there was **nothing left to do**. Before its attainment, there was **nothing it did not require**. From antiquity to now, none have succeeded without **vast knowledge, broad understanding, and unremitting effort**.

**Illuminating the Daoist Three Sovereigns’ Text and the Five Peaks’ True Forms Diagram**

I have heard that when entering mountains to refine the **Elixir**, seeking the **Dao** in **purity and stillness**, the **Three Sovereigns’ Text** and the **Five Peaks’ True Forms Diagram**—when summoning **mountain spirits**, consulting the **Ghost Ledger**, and questioning the **local deities, mountain officials, and marsh marshals**—one comes to know the **monsters of wood and stone**, the **essences of mountains and rivers**, which dare not approach to test mortals.

It is like the **Buddhist *Prajñāpāramitā Sūtra***, where sacred sites are marked: knowing this, one recognizes the **stūpa**, and all malevolent beings naturally retreat. The **Daoists** who possess this **Text** govern its efficacy, hence it is said that the **most sacred of Daoist scriptures** is not lightly bestowed. Those who receive it transmit it only once every **forty years**. The transmitter swears an oath with **blood**, pledging his very life.

The **Five Sacred Peaks** all possess this **Text**, hidden in **secluded stone chambers**. Those destined to attain the **Dao**, with **sincere devotion**, will have the **mountain spirits** reveal it. If one obtains this **Text**, it must be placed in its proper location. Whatever is undertaken, one must first **know it as reverently as serving one’s parents and sovereign**.

The **Scripture** states:
*"If a household possesses the **Three Sovereigns’ Text**, it repels all **malevolent spirits, poisons, epidemics, and calamities**. If one is afflicted with illness, near death, and yet believes in the **Dao**, and with sincere heart invokes this **Text**, they shall be revived."*

The **Daoist** who seeks **transcendence**, invoking this **Text** into the mountains, repels **tigers and mountain spirits**; the **Five Poisons and a hundred malevolences** dare not approach. One may **cross rivers and seas**, repelling **dragon serpents and winds**. Its methods can **transform and manifest efficacy**, regardless of **auspicious or inauspicious omens, calamities, or retributions**.

Those who seek verification must **observe strict purity for a hundred days**, then according to the method, summon the **gods of destiny, the Year God, the Day God, the Five Peaks, the Four Seas, and the deities of the soil and grain**. They will appear in human form, allowing one to inquire into **fortune and misfortune, safety and peril**, and the **origins of a sick person’s afflictions**.

Among **Daoists**, if one obtains this **Text** but lacks **benevolence, righteousness, and compassion**, or is not **pure and upright**, even if they possess it, **calamity will swiftly befall them**. It must not be treated lightly.

Thus, in antiquity, the **Yellow Emperor** bound himself to **Qingqiu Hill**, passed through **Feng Mountain**, and met **Master Purple Symbol**, receiving the **Inner Text of the Three Sovereigns**, using it to **forcefully summon ten thousand spirits**—this is the very **Text** in question.

**Illuminating the Verification of Talismans in Invoking Spirits**

I have examined: when entering mountains to refine the **Elixir**, the most **critical method** to repel **mountain and river deities, temple spirits, and ghosts** is through **talismans**. The **Great Elixir** and **divine medicines** also rely on the **power of talismans** for protection.

The **talismanic methods** are all **celestial texts** from **Laozi**; **Laozi** received them from **divine humans**. When people use them and find them slightly effective, it is because the **transcriptions lack proper transmission**, leading to many errors. Additionally, their **faith is not sincere**, and they merely apply them without devotion.

Moreover, even **common scriptures** contain identical characters, where **Spring and Autumn Annals** scholars like **Du Yu** corrected errors in **five instances**, and **Sima Qian’s *Records of the Grand Historian*** was emended by **Zi Xia** for **Yǐ-Hài** mistakes.

This is the **talismanic method** as well.

The saying goes: *"Three transcriptions of a book, and the fish becomes Lu; the emperor becomes a tiger seven."* Scholars merely follow the sound of the characters, and when faced with questions of length or stroke count, they often introduce errors. As for **talismans**, their strokes and patterns are not like ordinary characters—mistakes cannot be detected, errors cannot be perceived, so no one knows whether they are correct or incorrect.

Yet, those who seek **long life** must first control the **medicine**, not rely on the **power of talismans** alone to achieve transcendence. Still, what must not be neglected is their ability to **ward off ghosts and repel malevolence**, assisting the gradual refinement of the **Great Elixir**.

For the **talisman of the Named Mountain**, summoning the **High Mountain Lord’s talisman**:
This **talisman** requires a ten-day fast. When summoning the **High Mountain Lord**, it removes the harm of tigers and wolves. The **talisman** is written in cinnabar on silk, measuring one *chi* two *cun* in both length and width. If written without proper breath and intent, it will be ineffective. Upon completion, burn incense, then at night in a quiet chamber, align it with the moon’s position, wear new clothing, and invoke it—its characters facing downward.

For using **talismans**, write large characters on the front of both shoulders, always maintaining a steady and reverent focus on their auspicious symbols. Place additional **talismans** at the **Six Jia Positions** (六甲), aligned with the **talisman**, along with the names of the **Immortal Maiden** (玉女), invoking the entity summoned. Gaze upward, visualize its form, and imagine beyond the eight directions, extending one’s **spirit** (神) to five *chi* in length. Clothing should follow the **Six Jia Scripture** (《六甲经》).

For the **Mountain Ascent Talisman**, the **Immortal** Chen Anshi, upon entering the mountain, wrote it on the **Jia-Yin day** (甲寅) on white silk. At night, facing the **Big Dipper** (北斗), he offered wine and dried meat, recited his name, and placed it inside his collar to **ward off malevolence** and **subdue a hundred ghosts**, repelling **noxious beasts** (老物), tigers, wolves, and venomous creatures. He wore it, set it upon his desk, and faced the **Big Dipper**.

This **talisman** has the same strokes as the previous one, differing only slightly in size and form, yet its efficacy remains identical. Thus, both are preserved here.

This **talisman** may be written in cinnabar on silk or plain white silk, worn on the left arm for men and the right for women, or placed at the dwelling’s four directions and four corners, as well as along the path one travels—four sets at each fifty *bu* interval—to **ward off all spirits**.

This **talisman** is also for repelling tigers and wolves. When moving or relocating the plaque, if one wishes to **ward off mountain spirits**, it should be used along the path.

I note: Those who practice the **Dao** often dwell in mountains and forests, which are dens of **noxious beasts** (老物), **poisonous snakes**, and **spirits and demons** (精怪魍魉). Without profound precautions and **warding methods**, one risks harming the **medicine**. In ancient times, the **Round Mound** (圆丘) was filled with great snakes, yet it also produced fine medicines. When the **Yellow Emperor** ascended the mountain to seek these **medicines**, the snakes became venomous. **Master Guangcheng** taught the **Yellow Emperor** to wear **雄黄** (male yellow, realgar), and all the snakes retreated. Thus, those who enter mountains to refine the **Great Elixir**, unaware of mountain entry **warding methods**, often face grave dangers. The saying goes: *"Beneath Mount Taihua, bones lie strewn like wolves’ tracks"*—this is because they know only one thing and cannot be fully prepared. Though they seek **life**, they hasten toward **death**.

Yet **Daoists** only grasp the **Great Principle** (大方) and neglect the **mountain entry warding methods**, which is no small matter. Though snakes vary in kind, the **locust snake** (蝗蛇) and the **green scaly snake** (青蛞) that bite humans are the most urgent. If not treated within a day, they kill. To save a victim, cut away the wounded flesh with a knife, cast it upon the ground—it resembles burning coals—and in moments, it withers completely, allowing the person to recover. However, these two snakes peak in venom during autumn, when they cannot prey on living things. They then bite their **large bamboo or wood**, causing them to wither and die—this is their way of **venting poison**. Thus, when ascending a mountain, fearing these snakes is paramount. One must first **prevent harm at home**, mastering the **warding methods** beforehand.

Focus on the **cycles of day and month**, as well as the **Vermilion Bird** (朱乌), **Black Tortoise** (玄武), **Green Dragon** (青龙), and **White Tiger** (白虎) to **guard one’s body**. Then, in the mountain’s grasslands, take the **Three Qis** (三气) on the left and seal them.

Walk through the mountain grass in sequence, and focus your mind to gather **Lún-qi** of a crimson hue, filling the air like clouds and mist for tens of miles around.

If traveling with companions—whether three or five—arrange them in a circle. Have each blow forth their **qi**, and even if they tread upon snakes, the serpents shall not move. Moreover, they shall not even be seen.

Additionally, carry five *liang* of **realgar**. Once you ascend the mountain, you need not fear the snakes. If bitten, apply realgar powder to the wound, and it shall heal.

Also, bind **dried realgar** and **aconite** behind your elbows, or burn the horns of oxen, sheep, or deer to focus the essence, or stuff the waxy secretion from **wild bees’ ears** into your ears, or place a **musk pellet** beneath your toenails—all are effective.

The **Hundred-Snakes-Warding Seal** and the **Talisman That Repels Tigers and Wolves**
These two divine seals are inscribed on **date pits** with a **bi** brush, arranged in a square formation, and offered with two prostrations before being worn. They are highly effective.

These are the seals taught by the **Yellow-Headed Maiden of the Vermilion Palace** and the **Jade Room**, which cause tigers and wolves to retreat without daring to approach.

This may also be written as a talisman in **vermilion script**, swallowed before entering the mountains to repel tigers and wolves. It may also be made into a seal.

From the *Jade Embryo Scripture*.

This talisman repels ghosts. When mixing medicines, hang it on all four sides to subdue them—its divine efficacy is great.

One version has seven curves at its center; the rest remains the same. If this is unknown, it is likely an error.

This is a **mountain-dwelling talisman**, inscribed on a **spirit board** and placed at the four doorposts. Tigers and wolves shall not come near.

All three talismans above are written in **vermilion script**.

One version of this talisman reads as follows:
This is the **Mountain-Opening Talisman**, made from **thousand-year-old hemp** (*Máimén Dòng*), twelve *cùn* long. Write this talisman in cinnabar and press it onto a great stone at a named mountain. The mountain gate shall open, and the **Treasure Scripture** shall emerge.

Gold and jade shall also appear before you.

*Thousand-year-old hemp* is **Heaven’s Mercy**—resembling a mouse’s ear—repelling ghosts and subduing spirits.

In deep, quiet mountains, construct a **cinnabar hall** at the four corners, hang **bell charms**, and prepare **ghost-repelling medicines** and **ghost-repelling talismans** on all sides before mixing medicines. Otherwise, ghosts and spirits will absorb the medicine’s essence, causing it to fail entirely. Thus, talismans and exorcisms must be prepared beforehand.

**Method for Ghost-Repelling Medicine**:
Take **illuminating sand**, realgar, orpiment, musk, aconite, white wood, **ghostvine**, and **devil’s archer**—each two *dōu*—and knead with honey.

From the *Yellow Emperor’s Jade Platform Diagram and Talisman*.

The minister notes: In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor visited **Éméi Mountain**, where he saw the **Celestial Sage** in the **Jade Hall**. He requested the **True Dao** and sought **immortality**.

The Celestial Sage, formerly the Yellow Emperor’s master, spoke thus:

*"All Daoists who seek immortality and wish to forge great medicines, refining the five stones, must dwell in secluded mountain retreats, never lingering in defiled or worldly places. If they violate this, the medicines they concoct will never succeed. Moreover, if they lack My **Jade Platform Secret Talisman**, even with other methods, nothing will come to fruition."*

The Celestial Sage further declared:

*"All Daoists who seek immortality and obtain this text shall soon attain the Dao. Those who wish to enter the mountains to study the Dao, forge the elixir, kindle the fire, and craft the medicine of longevity must first observe a fast for one hundred and fifty days. Then, on auspicious days—**Wangxiang Days** and **Five-Peace Days**—when the hour does not clash, they must use fragrant bathwater, **well-blossom water**, twelve *shēng* of white grain flour, one *liǎng* of cinnabar, one *dōu* of white honey, and one *dōu* of mulberry twig ash.

First, take one *dōu* of the five-scented bathwater, then mix the flour, cinnabar, honey, and ash together with a **tao-twig stirrer**—three inches long—stirring it twenty-one times in the **Yang-direction**. Place the mixture in a quiet chamber.

Take the **Platform Talisman**, and with a clear, tranquil mind, use a fresh brush to inscribe it according to the prescribed method, placing it at the edge of the bath basin, facing the corresponding direction.

Take a copper vessel and suspend the **Central Talisman** above the bath, three *chǐ* from the water. After adjusting one’s breath and stepping outside, perform the **Yǔ-bù steps**, hold one’s breath, then re-enter the chamber. Slowly release the breath near the bath basin, regulate one’s breathing to settle, close the eyes, press the palms together, stand upright and composed. First, bow seven times in homage to the **East Direction Talisman**, then fourteen times to the **South Direction Talisman**, twenty-one times to the **West Direction Talisman**, thirty-five times to the **North Direction Talisman**, and finally, facing the door with feet aligned, bow forty-nine times to the talisman above the bath basin. Then, bathe from head to toe in the bathwater, ensuring inner and outer purity with its fragrance.

Once the bathwater is exhausted, don fresh garments. Bind the **Square Talisman** to the left arm, place the **West Direction Talisman** on the right arm, the **South Direction Talisman** before the heart, the **North Direction Talisman** on the back, and swallow the **Central Talisman**.

Afterward, observe strict purity for seven more days in a secluded chamber. The **Five Cloud Divine Might** will descend, its light illuminating the room, radiating within and without. Do not be alarmed—remain steadfast in **Oneness**, for the Divine Presence will manifest. There may be pressure, and soon its form will vanish—this is the supreme verification.

Or, if questioned by others, the radiant light itself shall be the proof.

Should these signs appear, the Dao will inevitably be attained. From that point onward, all endeavors will succeed.

For those who seek longevity, transcend the world, transmute the five stones, ingest wondrous medicines, reverse aging, or ascend as immortals—if they do not adhere to the talismanic method, all their efforts will fail. They will waste their labor, achieving nothing. Slander will arise, claiming no Daoist methods exist. Though they employ various other techniques—whether through **prohibitive incantations**, **single talismans**, or medicines—they will still fail without this method. There is no doubt.

Moreover, if one forges medicines not to cultivate the Dao or nurture the True Self, but for worldly gain and glory—pursuing **yellow and white alchemy**—there will be no success. This will invite the disdain of the numinous, provoke the wrath of spirits, invite calamities, and bring misfortune. Not only will the medicines be wasted, but poverty will follow. How can this be beneficial? It will only shorten one’s life and bring ruin. Be cautious and reflect deeply.

Let no Daoist recklessly transmit this to the unworthy, nor speak of it to others. Those without shared aspiration must not lightly divulge it."*

「This is the first *Huánglǎo Yútái* divine talisman, governing the **Southern Quarter**. Use **black** as the base, and **white flowers** as the design. The second *Huánglǎo Yútái* divine talisman governs the **Western Quarter**, with **white** as the base and **yellow flowers** as the design. There is a central talisman with the character *fù* (父) beneath its core; when making this talisman, the rest follow the same pattern.

The third *Huánglǎo Yútái* divine talisman governs the **Northern Quarter**, with **indigo** as the base and **blue flowers** as the design. The fourth *Huánglǎo Yútái* divine talisman governs the **Central Quarter**, with **yellow** as the base and **black flowers** as the design. #¹ The fifth *Huánglǎo Yútái* divine talisman governs the **Eastern Quarter**, with **blue** as the base and **crimson flowers** as the design.

The *Huángrén* (皇人) says: *"For any Daoist who wishes to employ these talismans, let them use the five colors as their seats, placing them according to the respective quarters. Each must be cleansed and purified. Only the Central Quarter’s divine elixir talisman is placed in a copper cauldron, with yellow as its seat, suspended above the cauldron three feet from the water—some say seven feet."*

*"The method for each seat is to make them square and equal in measure, then to wrap them in a pouch of equal size, binding them as described above. For the Central Quarter, burn the talisman along with its seat and consume the ashes mixed with water. The Huángrén says: ‘In the mountains, when refining elixirs or tending the furnace, one must first obtain my talisman. Only then will the Dao be fulfilled. When setting up the furnace and placing the *áo* (鳌) beneath it, choose a day when the *Ruler* (王) and *Minister* (相) *Stems* (支) and *Branches* (干) do not clash. From each of the four sides of the furnace, measure three feet outward, and bury it five inches deep. Then take the Central Quarter talisman and bury it beneath the furnace, seven feet deep. Afterward, at the furnace’s placement, swallow the Central Quarter talisman along with it. Then wear the talisman corresponding to one’s own person, and the numinous immortals will aid and protect you—keep it secret."*

*"All great named mountains possess divine *zhī* (芝), sacred grasses, and wondrous elixirs of the immortals. Without a secret method, acquiring them is difficult. Even if one finds them, they will vanish in an instant. Daoists seeking these elixirs should first observe strict fasting, bathe in fragrant waters, and wear these five-colored *Yútái* divine talismans. Whatever divine *zhī* (芝) or sacred grasses they encounter, they may take as they wish. All such plants are guarded by tigers, wolves, dragons, serpents, great *mǎng* (蟒), and myriad poisons, and the Daoist must wear the talismans when entering or leaving. Then all mountain spirits and numinous beings will retreat and conceal themselves, turning their gaze upon the Daoist, who will also be augmented in protection."*

*"The completion of the fifth scroll of the *Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Divine Elixir Canon* (黄帝九鼎神丹经诀卷之五). The beginning of the sixth scroll of the *Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Divine Elixir Canon* (黄帝九鼎神丹经诀卷之六).*

The *Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Divine Elixir Canon* (黄帝九鼎神丹经诀) elucidates the profound Dao of seeking the divine *shéndān* (神丹) for universal benefit.

The humble servant hears that among all forms created between Heaven and Earth, humanity is the most precious. Yet, bound by the swift decay of aging and death, proper cultivation sustains life, while neglect hastens its end. Life is a treasure to be cherished, death a fate to be dreaded. Thus, the Daoist tradition holds this Way of longevity as its most sacred and weighty pursuit—none surpasses this method of attaining eternal life.

If the elixirs and methods of longevity are sought, none exceeds the Dao of the divine *shéndān*. Should this Dao be perfected yet fail to grant longevity, then never in antiquity was there such a Way.

The value of life to oneself is indeed immense. When comparing worth, even the highest noble rank—public duke or great marquis—cannot measure its worth. When weighing its importance, even vast rewards and territorial grants cannot exchange it. A lifespan of one hundred years comprises over three thousand days; in youth and weakness, one knows little joy, while in old age, pleasures grow insipid. The span between vigor and decrepitude, excluding the occasional decades of moderate health, often amounts to mere thousands of days—flitting like lightning or swift as a galloping steed. Of those who reach a hundred years, only one in ten thousand does so without affliction. Yet even if one survives, sorrow, hardship, and turmoil soon follow. Therefore, the noble pursuit of the worthy must first secure the matter of longevity, so that they may act freely, without praying for transcendence, and enjoy pleasures with others, equaling the lifespan of *Péngzǔ* (彭祖) or the distant longevity of *Shǎojiūn* (少君). Thus, they may nurture their families, hold office, and neither fail in their duties to superiors and inferiors nor forfeit the joys of human nature. This is indeed a grand endeavor.

Yet the Way of longevity must rely upon elixirs, and among elixirs, the divine *shéndān* holds supreme mastery—an unparalleled method as proclaimed by the Yellow Emperor. Yet scholars misinterpret, and seekers err in their pursuit. They claim the Dao lies in mere textual prescriptions, that the divine *shéndān* is but a mundane elixir for vitality and transcendence. Following such doctrines blindly, they achieve nothing in a hundred attempts.

This failure is not due to the elixirs themselves lacking efficacy, but because the Dao is not recorded. Hence, *Liú Xiàng* (刘向) did not attain mastery in the Han dynasty, and *Táo Míng* (陶明) found no verification in the Liang era. Only those of keen intelligence and profound wisdom, whose talents suffice to undertake such labors, who endure arduous trials, accumulate through hardship, and persistently refine their inner cultivation and outer alchemy—commencing with resolve and completing with steadfastness, neither wavering nor straying—who do not await the evening’s dawn for morning’s work nor stand in expectation of seated results, who are not deterred by slander nor weary of prolonged effort, who endure initial hardship only to reap final ease—can they achieve this.

It is akin to a sovereign governing a state or a general confronting an enemy: external conquest relies on martial strategy, while internal governance depends on wise counsel. Thus, the human body mirrors a realm: the chest and abdomen are its palaces, the limbs its officials, the spirit its sovereign, the blood its ministers, and the *qi* its people. The cultivation of the body is like ruling a state; cherishing one’s people preserves the realm, while firming one’s *qi* safeguards the spirit. To preserve the spirit, one must not deplete the *qi*; to preserve the realm, one must not scatter the people. Human *qi* is difficult to nourish yet easily endangered, hard to purify yet easily turbid.

Thus, one must uphold the balance of virtue and authority to safeguard the state, and curb desires to fortify the blood and *qi*, only then may one achieve the ultimate purpose. When the Way of longevity is perfected, a hundred calamities are averted, and years of life are extended.

Compare this to wood such as willow and poplar—easily grown things. Even if planted shallowly or improperly, they flourish with little care: sometimes they thrive, sometimes they wither, sometimes they sway, sometimes they are uprooted. Though nourished with fertile soil and bathed in spring’s dew, they do not perish swiftly, for their roots rot slowly, and their sprouts struggle to emerge, their vital fluids unable to coalesce into sustaining *qi*. Yet the human body is fragile, easily injured and hard to cultivate. When set against these two, it falls far short.

The causes of its destruction are manifold, yet its afflictions surpass even the violent uprooting or swaying of willow and poplar. The vital *qi* is worn down by exhaustion, the *hóngwèi* (荣卫) system dissipates from overuse, the form is consumed by *jiānáo* (煎熬), and the natural balance is eroded by excessive strain. Improper diet, indulgence in luxury, exposure to wind and damp, or the onset of disease—when such misfortunes arise, some entrust their fate to herbal remedies, others to the aid of spirit-mediums. Yet this is like digging wells for water on high ground or plowing barren fields for sustenance when famished.

What can herbs truly restore? What power do gods and spirits possess to heal? If life could be prolonged by herbs and illness dispelled by rites, then physicians would all live forever, and shamans would never fall ill.

Thus the Canon states: *"It is not in the rites of sacrifice to Qi (杞) nor in invoking the gods and spirits with vows and incantations. The Way of longevity lies solely in the divine *shéndān*."* Knowing this is not easy; practicing it is even harder. Yet among those who seek the Dao, few advance while many retreat. Many claim to understand, but few achieve the ultimate purpose. It is not the Dao that is hard to hear, nor the practice that is difficult—it is the perseverance to its end that is arduous. Without utmost clarity, one cannot discern it; without tireless effort, one cannot master it. If it were easy to attain, then the Han Emperor *Wǔ* (武) would have secured it through his encounter with *Shǎojiūn* (少君). If it were easy to comprehend, then *Liú Xiàng* (刘向) would have unlocked it from the *Zhěnzhōng* (枕中) text.

Therefore, the Yellow Emperor ascended *Kēngdōng* (崆峒) to seek the Dao, and *Xià Yǔ* (夏禹) enshrined the golden tablets in a jade casket. Some attained their purpose and ascended, others perfected the Way and departed as immortals—evidence of their success is clear, and their methods flourished among earlier practitioners.

The true method of longevity is found only in the divine *shéndān*. Using *dān* (丹) as gold, gold as the vessel, and the vessel as its repository, one nourishes the body by ingestion, gradually dissolving the intestines and stomach, irrigating the *hóngwèi* (荣卫) system, and drawing upon its utmost strength and integrity to arrest the passage of time.

Yet gold, though called the supreme treasure, varies in quality. Some gold carries poison, and its formation inevitably harms the body. Others, such as *zhìjīn* (铆金) or *gāngjīn* (刚金), possess a naturally harsh and toxic nature, also damaging to the body.

There are three: the third, where *dān* and gold undergo divine transformation and marvelous refinement, its power extending longevity.

Yet the upper-grade minerals, though called *dān*, often contain toxic *qi*, and their formation inevitably harms the body.
Like one parched drinking pigeon broth, or starving feasting on poisoned dried meat—seeking gain yet causing harm, the damage is indeed grave.

In the present age, many scholars and seekers inquire about different methods, yet from the time of **Róngchéng** (容成) to the era of **Péngzǔ** (彭祖), their observations vary. Some have not yet attained perfection, possessing gold but failing to remove its poison, or unable to refine it into true gold.
Even if they succeed, the result is useless.
If they do not carefully refine and select with utmost precision, all efforts are in vain.

Time cannot be delayed—do not wait until the day is over.
All things before life, only longevity is the most precious.
I humbly pray that the **Holy Sage** (圣聪) may heed this and harmonize with the **Heaven-Earth** (天地).

The **Nine *Dān*** are listed below:
1. **Dānhuá** (丹华)
2. **Shénfú** (神符)
3. **Shéndān** (神丹)
4. **Huándān** (还丹)
5. **Èdān** (饵丹)
6. **Liàndān** (炼丹)
7. **Rǔdān** (柔丹)
8. **Fúdān** (伏丹)
9. **Hándān** (寒丹)

These are merely the names of the **Nine *Dān***, their meanings and elucidations appearing in the subsequent volumes.

**The Canon of the Divine *Shéndān* in the Nine Cauldrons by the Yellow Emperor**—Volume 6 ends.
**The Canon of the Divine *Shéndān* in the Nine Cauldrons by the Yellow Emperor**—Volume 7 begins.

The **Canon of the Divine *Shéndān* in the Nine Cauldrons by the Yellow Emperor**—Volume 7 continues:

**Clarifying the Guarding of Oneness, Sealing Evil, and the Construction of the *Dān* House and Cauldron**

The subject humbly submits: In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor received the *Dān* scriptures from Xuánnǚ and sought the **True Oneness** (*Zhēnyī*) from the **Royal Sage** (*Huángrén*). Therefore, it is said that the **elixir of immortality** is solely the **golden *dān***, and the **preservation of form and repulsion of evil** depend entirely on the **True Oneness**. The ancients held this in the highest regard.

The **Daoist scriptures** state: The *Jiǔ Zhuǎn Jīndān Jīng* and the **Precepts of Guarding Oneness** are all kept within the **Five Cities of Kunlun**, sealed in **jade cases**, inscribed with **golden tablets**, bound with **purple reishi**, and stamped with the **Central Seal**—these are the supreme secrets of the **Sage of the Highest Virtue**.

The reasons why mortals die are sixfold:
1. **Depletion** (*Sǔn*)
2. **Aging** (*Lǎo*)
3. **The onset of a hundred ailments**
4. **Poison and malevolence**
5. **Harm by malevolent *qi***
6. **Assault by wind and pestilence**

For those who wish to repel evil, they must practice **guiding and drawing** (*Dǎoyǐn*), **circulating *qi***, **returning essence to nourish the brain**, **moderate diet and drink**, **regulated activity and rest**, **meditation on the Oneness**, **wearing talismans and seals**, and **avoiding taboos**. This can ward off malevolence and prevent misfortune, but it cannot prolong life or dispel all diseases.

If one encounters **violent ghosts, raging malevolence, untimely death, or the spread of pestilence and poison**, and the **great elixir is not yet complete**, then these methods alone cannot repel them.

It is like one who removes armor and rain gear—useful only in battle or storms. If there is no war or impending rain, then having or not having them makes no difference.

If arrows and blades clash, swords and spears cross, then it is like being unarmored in battle. If torrential rain pours down or snow blankets the sky, then one knows the danger of standing exposed.

If illness has not yet healed, the **divine *dān*** is not yet refined, one’s lifespan is uncertain, and malevolent forces have not been dispelled, then the **Way of Guarding Oneness** must be known.

**Entering the mountains to guard Oneness**, and the hundred poisons dare not approach. No other methods are needed—the malevolent forces will depart on their own. The method follows, though its details are found in the **Royal Sage’s *Sān Yī* scroll**, so they are not transcribed here.

**Method for Building a Flying *Dān* House**
First, select a deep mountain by flowing water, a secluded cliffside, where humans and beasts are scarce. Hang **talismans and seals**, purify the heart, and observe strict fasting. Remove three *cùn* of old soil from the ground, then fill with fresh earth and level it. Raise a foundation three *chi* high, avoiding old mounds or ruins.

The house should be three *zhàng* long and one *zhàng* six *chǐ* wide, meticulously constructed with good materials, covered with fine grass, and plastered inside and out to be sturdy and sealed. The door should face due south, with two doorways, each four *chǐ* wide, to be closed at dusk. The firelight and the master’s presence determine its use.

Within the house, place the **cauldron** at the center, concealed by a dense barrier, with high walls to prevent intrusion. If the walls are not high or sturdy, heavy rain will breach them—these are the old methods, but current practice differs.

If the site is an **absolute cliffside** or a **prime waterfront location**, being deep in the mountains, one should not overwork the land. Only remove decayed materials, fill with good soil, bury the **seal beneath the cauldron**, and firmly compact it. The *Dān* scriptures also state: To refine the **divine *dān***, one must be in **deep mountains or vast marshes**, or in **remote villages and wilds where no one resides**. If refining is done among people, one must build **high walls and thick barriers**, ensuring no sight or sound passes between inside and out. The space should be small, accommodating only two or three people at most.

First, observe seven days of fasting, bathe with **five fragrant herbs**, and maintain absolute purity—avoid passing through or associating with **impure or mourning households**.

**Method for Repelling Malevolent Ghosts, Evil Spirits, and Thieves in Mountain Dwellings**
When residing in the mountains and fearing malevolent ghosts, take with your **left hand** a **Green Dragon** talisman, fold it in half, and place it beneath the **Blue Star** position. Pass through the **Bright Hall**, enter the **Yin Realm**, and take **Yǔbù** steps while reciting:
*"Nògāo Tàiyīn Jiāngjūn, open only for your descendant [name], and close to outsiders. Those who see this seal shall think it firewood. Those who do not see it shall think it unnatural."*

Then break the accompanying grass and place it on the ground. With your left hand, take soil and transfer it to your **person’s center** (*rénzhōng*). With your right hand, hold the grass to shield yourself, and place your left hand over your heart. Take **Yǔbù** steps until reaching the **Six *Gēng*** position, then hold your breath and stand still—thus, ghosts cannot see you.

The **Six *Jiǎ*** correspond to the **Green Dragon**, the **Six *Yǐ*** to the **Blue Star**, the **Six *Bǐng*** to the **Bright Hall**, and the **Six *Dīng*** to the **Yin Realm**.

When refining **immortal medicines**, use the **Four Seasons’ Sovereign and Minister days**, ensuring the day and hour do not clash. The sky must be clear, the sun and moon bright, and the *qi* tranquil—only then may work begin.

When taking **elixir medicines**, avoid the **five days of yin weather, snow, wind, rain, or heavy dew**. Medicines must be taken in a **tranquil place** for swift and powerful effects.

When consuming elixirs, be cautious of **spicy foods, meat, strong odors, sexual activity, impurity, attending funerals, or mourning**—these are all **great prohibitions**.

This is the **Great Prohibition Talisman for Refining the Supreme Elixir** by **Hújiāngzǐ**.

The courtyard’s **refining chamber** should be **twelve *bù* square**, with a height of **two *zhàng* four *chǐ***. The **south-facing door** should have a **barred gate** in front, ensuring a **continuous stream of water** flows by.

**Bundle the Days and Months.**
Wrap the chamber’s exterior with fragrant clay, sealing each side with a talisman for the **Master Elixir**.

Within the chamber, erect the **Five Sacred Peaks** and **Three Celestial Platforms**. Beneath the western wall, construct a tiered altar, placing all medicinal herbs and sacred elixir scriptures upon it, arranged in order. Let the **Living Medicine Envoys** guard them—then no malevolent *qi* shall dare intrude.

**Method for the Tortoise Foundation:**
At the chamber’s center, fashion a tortoise (*áo*), its mouth facing west. Repair it with fine *bóshí* stone, then grind bitter wine and flowing western water with fine white clay, mixing in *Pútái* clay to seal it. Within the tortoise, install an iron tripod—its legs forged from raw iron, the better. Place the elixir cauldron upon the tripod, ensuring it sits perfectly centered, neither tilted nor askew. On all four sides, maintain a uniform distance of **one *cùn* and a half** from the tortoise’s edge, so that the tortoise rises **two *cùn*** above the cauldron’s rim, encircling it. Constantly feed it with husk-fed fire to maintain even heat, lest the flames wax too strong or too weak.

**Method for the Five Sacred Peaks and Three Celestial Platforms:**
First, shape the forms of the **Five Sacred Peaks**, then within their center, erect the **Three Celestial Platforms**. Construct them with pottery and stone, sealing them with fragrant clay. Some scriptures prescribe alternate methods. If one cannot erect the Five Peaks and Three Platforms, then a **Talisman Chamber** must suffice. Within this chamber, place the tortoise, its perimeter spanning **seven *chǐ*** in breadth. Build it with pottery and stone, using yellow cinnabar clay to seal its exterior.

**Method for the Iron Cauldron and Martial Seal:**
Measure the cauldron’s circumference according to its size, fitting it with three legs, each **one *chǐ* and six *cùn*** high.

**Method for the Earthen Cauldron:**
*The humble servant notes:* The divine vessels for refining elixirs and merging medicines are made of earth, not iron, for the ancients knew well—though diagrams were cast, they were never forged. For the five metals bear poison, and cannot be used recklessly. Thus, in the **Great Elixir Methods**, there is no place for iron implements. Moreover, crafting an earthen cauldron is most difficult—hair’s breadth imperfections cause medicines to scatter and spill. From ancient times, those who labored for years, only to fail in old age, did so because of this flaw. The sages held it in such reverence that they never transmitted the method.

Yet, when making an earthen cauldron, one must use **Six-to-One Clay**—six parts to one, totaling seven. The sages concealed this, hence the name *liùyī* (六一). **Method for Six-to-One Clay:**
Take **alum stone, *rùshí* (誉石), martial salt, and *lǔxián* (卤咸)*,* and roast them for twenty days. Then gather **western-sea facing-left *mǔlì* (牡蛎), red stone pitch, and talc**. Seven substances in total—more or less as needed. Pound them **ten thousand strokes**, sift the finest powder, and knead with **one-hundred-day bitter wine** to form clay pellets.

Those who use elixirs all speak of *liùyī*, though not all employ all seven. Each has their own method, but the key is securing its firmness. For instance, in the **Seven Treasures Unending Pill** transmitted by *Zuǒyuán Fàng* to *Hújiāngzǐ*, the earthen cauldron’s clay is made with **purple quartz, white stone, red stone pitch, *mǔlì* powder, and white talc**, each **twenty *jīn***—pounded and kneaded with a marvelous technique, recorded here among the *liùyī* methods, though only three are used.

Moreover, though *liùyī* serves as cauldron clay, its virtues are profound: **alum stone** lightens the body, strengthens bones, and prolongs life; ***rùshí*** sharpens vision, lowers *qi*, benefits the liver, and quells thirst; **martial salt** repels venomous creatures and fortifies flesh; ***lǔxián*** dispels internal heat and eases nausea, wheezing, and fullness; **red stone pitch** enhances wisdom, banishes hunger, lightens the body, and extends years; **white talc** lightens the body, lengthens life, endures hunger, and quenches thirst. Thus, a cauldron of this clay already carries the power of longevity—hence the ancients’ reliance on it. First, they shaped the **red-earth cauldron** as its skeletal frame, then repeatedly coated it with *liùyī* clay, and finally applied **black-and-yellow elixir** to seal it. Thus, the sequence is as follows:

**Method for the Red-Earth Cauldron:**
Take **parrot-liver red earth**, yellow in hue, and pound it finely through silk, then steam it until midday. Mix with thin wine to form clay, pounding until perfectly refined. Mold **three small and six large** earthen cauldrons—this is the proper count. Alternatively, make additional sets for testing dryness and moisture. Adjust the size according to the medicine’s quantity. Ensure the walls are **five *fèn*** thick, then dry them in shade for **thirty days**. The smaller hold **eight or nine *shēng***, the larger **one and a half *dòu***. Some say to make them **three *fèn*** thick, then bake until utterly dry.

Next, take **thirty *jīn* of *xièsè* (懈树) bark**, finely chopped, and boil it in **three *dàn* of water** for a day. Strain and reduce to **one *shēng***, its color a deep black—this is called *xièsè* lacquer. Apply it to the cauldron’s exterior according to its number, reinforcing its strength so that it neither cracks nor shatters in fire. This is the **secret technique for the divine elixir’s earthen cauldron**.

**Method for the Six-to-One Clay in Forging the Elixir Furnace:**
Take **three hundred *jīn* of eastern-sea facing-left *mǔlì***, scrape away the flesh, and pound it in an iron mortar. Sift through silk into a basin, rinsing with water until it resembles white wine. Stir repeatedly for hundreds of strokes, then let it settle overnight. Decant the water, retaining only the finest sediment. Dry it under the sun. The coarser residue is repounded and resifted as before, then placed in an iron vessel and roasted atop the tortoise’s shell with blazing woodfire for **twenty days**, until its color matches the flame. Let it cool for a day, then resift through silk. Knead with **one-hundred-day medicinal wine** to form clay, applying it with a brush of sheep’s hair to coat the earthen cauldron.

Next, take **native *rùshí*, alum stone, talc, red stone pitch, martial salt, and *lǔxián***, each in equal measure, and pound them without sifting. Roast them for twenty days as well. Then, mix the previously prepared *mǔlì* powder with these seven substances, binding them with vinegar to form clay for coating the cauldron. **One hundred *jīn* of *mǔlì* powder** may suffice, with each of the seven ingredients measured at **two *jīn*** apiece.

「The Method of Sealing with *Mǔlì* Clay

Take fine yellow soil like tallow, expose it to the sun until dry, then pound and sift it, washing with water as in the preparation of *mǔlì* powder. After drying, break it into pieces the size of plums, then roast it with fierce fire for three days until it turns uniformly red like cinnabar. Let it cool completely, then pound and sift it three times, adding one *jīn* of yellow cinnabar (*huángdān*) and one *jīn* of paper ash (*zhǐtū*). Soak it thoroughly, then mix with five *jīn* of *ājiāo* (donkey-hide glue) decoction until sufficient, forming clay with paper ash. Pound it three thousand times, steam it in porcelain for half a day, and apply it to coat the *Liùyī* clay.

**The Method of Sealing the Cinnabar Cauldron with Clay**
Take a red-earth cauldron, first coating its exterior with *mǔlì* clay, five *fēn* thick on the surface and three *fēn* thick on the sides, letting it dry in shade for ten days until thoroughly desiccated. Then apply the *Liùyī* clay again, two *fēn* thick, with five *fēn* thickness on both surface and sides. This is the method of applying *Liùyī* clay without coating the surface.

**Coating the Cauldron**
During the first ten days of coating, the thickness should be five *fēn*. Once coating is complete, let it dry in shade for ten days, then expose it to the sun for another ten days. This ensures the inner and outer layers are each five *fēn* thick, though still thin overall—just twenty days in total.

**The Method of Coating with *Mǔlì* Clay**
The coating process should follow the same duration as the *Liùyī* clay method.

**Sealing the Cinnabar Furnace with *Liùyī* Clay**
After placing the medicine inside, first coat the two cauldron mouths with *Liùyī* clay before sealing them. Then apply *Liùyī* clay to the outer edges, gradually increasing the thickness. Once dry, coat it again, making it over one *cùn* thick, ensuring it is firm and sealed. Next, apply the *Mǔlì* divine clay uniformly, with a thickness of six to seven *fēn* on top—this is ideal.

**Sealing to Form the Cauldron’s Shape**
Seal it so that the cauldron resembles an inverted basin, maintaining its proper goose-egg shape. This is the sealing method.

If not done thus, the *Liùyī* clay, when exposed to fire, will lose its potency, causing the medicine’s essence to dissipate and the seal to crack. Cracks will allow the cinnabar’s essence to escape.

The ancient sage **Ānqī Shī** and **Guǎngchéng Zhàngrén** practiced together for over thirty years. Though Ānqī Shī obtained the cinnabar elixir (*dānjīng*) and was taught the various methods of refining, he was not given this crucial sealing technique. Even after nine refineries, he failed, and only after repeatedly begging did he receive this divine clay method—once applied, the elixir succeeded, and he ascended to **Tàiqīng** (the Supreme Purity).

**The Official’s Reflection**
This method makes sense. I have always doubted why cinnabar elixirs use clay made with horse or sheep wool—wool burns when exposed to fire, leaving hollow spaces. These gaps prevent the clay from sealing properly, allowing the medicine’s *qi* to escape.

When applying the clay, ensure it is completely dry before heating. If still slightly damp, it will crack upon exposure to heat.

Alternatively, one may use **tún clay** (a separate clay sample) to coat other objects, following the same measurements as the cauldron. After testing, remove the sample and examine the cauldron’s seal—whether it is thoroughly dry or not. You may also test by igniting it, observing the cauldron’s readiness.

This method is most critical: after shade-drying, expose it to the sun for ten days. The same applies for those who have already roasted it.

Even if clay objects have dried in shade for hundreds of days, upon heating they will inevitably soften. The presence of vinegar (*cù*) in this clay ensures flexibility, so it must be applied to small cauldrons and tested through multiple roastings.

**The Clay Application Method**
First coat the interior, then the exterior. Construct a lattice framework to guide placement, ensuring uniform thickness and measurements. If preparing round clay samples, adjust the mixture so that it dries evenly—do not let it dry directly in one place. Instead, stop and reposition it. If it rains, place the drying clay indoors near a fire to keep it warm. Since drying time is fixed, do not wait for it to dry naturally, or the schedule will be lost.

**The Method of Mixing Clay**
The clay must be thoroughly kneaded. Use a brush of sheep’s wool, first cleaning the bristles with ash and water, then arranging them in bundles for use.

**The Method of Mixing Clay with Wine**
For mixing *Liùyī* and similar clays, use pure, unrefined wine (*chúnjiǔ*)—no need for *huá pò* (medicinal wine from the *Huáshāng* text). If preparing *jīnshā* (golden sand), follow the established methods; this is not required here.

The scriptures state: *“One-hundred-day”* preparations must be completed within the full hundred days. If extended beyond this period—even to multiple years—it is better.

**The Method of Pounding Medicinal Substances**
For fine sifting of medicinal powders, silk is superior to grinding with a mortar. No further refining is needed.

**The Method of the *Hújiāngzǐ* (Vulpes Vulpes) Divine Cauldron**
Take red-yellow soil from the southern regions, thoroughly washing away impurities, and refine it until its hardness and softness are balanced.

First, shape the cauldron to a depth of seven *cùn* and a width of one *chǐ* two *cùn*, ensuring its edges are even and its walls uniformly thick at one *cùn*. The top and bottom should also be level. All subsequent cinnabar cauldrons should follow this standard.

Adjust size according to the quantity of medicine, forming them all at once. Let them dry in shade for one month, then place them inside an earthen furnace. The cauldron should be positioned first with the **wén** (civil, yin) method, then with the **wǔ** (martial, yang) method.

Once the fire has diminished slightly, remove the cauldron and place it in a clean chamber, ensuring no impurities remain. If the cauldron is not roasted, the process will be more arduous.

**The Method of Mixing *Hújiāngzǐ* Divine Cauldron Clay**
For the divine elixir cauldron, take ten *jīn* of **xuányín** (black silver, mercuric sulfide), one *jīn* of **bìbái** (white bismuth), and five *jīn* of **sān zhuǎn qiānhuáng** (three-time sublimated lead oxide). Layer them in the cauldron, then roast from sunrise to sunset until the lead essence fully emerges, resembling molten gold—this is called **xuánhuáng** (black-yellow elixir).

For the clay mixture: take one *jīn* each of **zǐshíyīng** (purple quartz), **bái shízhī** (white stone pitch), *mǔlì* powder, and **bái huáshí** (white talc). Pound and sift each separately, then knead with the juice of **yīnshòu xuánjīng** (dark animal essence) until pliable. Form into round cakes, dry them in the sun, then stack and roast in a furnace for ten days and nights. Once the fire is extinguished, cover and let it rest for another ten days.

Reknead the cakes, then place them in an iron mortar and pound separately until fine. Add **róngyán** (martial salt) and **lǔxián** (alum), mixing with water until thoroughly moistened. Then blend with *huá pò* (medicinal wine) and simmer into clay. Apply this clay to the cauldron, letting it dry before adding more layers—each layer should be about one *fēn* thick. Three applications suffice.

For coating earthen cauldrons with **xuánhuáng** clay, each layer should be only one *fēn* thick—this is the most divine and effective method.

Always inspect the clay’s surface, ensuring no cracks or exposed fibers. The key is to seal it firmly and securely.

**The Method of Preparing the Divine Cauldron’s *Xuánhuáng* Medicine**
Use ten *jīn* of **xuányín** (black silver), one *jīn* of **bìbái** (white bismuth), and five *jīn* of **sān zhuǎn qiānhuáng** (three-time sublimated lead oxide). Layer and place them in the earthen cauldron, then roast with fierce fire from sunrise to sunset until the lead essence fully emerges, appearing like molten gold—this is the **xuánhuáng** (black-yellow elixir).」

Also known as **Fēiqīng** (Flying Light) and **Fēiliú** (Flying Flow).
Take **húfěn** (arsenic trioxide), and also roast it in an iron vessel until it turns golden—equal in grade to **xuánhuáng** (black-yellow elixir). Pound it ten thousand times, mix with **zuǒwèi** (left flavor), and pound until it becomes a paste.

**The following methods for the Divine Cauldron’s pastes and medicines are not part of the Nine Elixirs’ True Essentials.**
Yet the humble subject has tested them and found them most wondrous, so they are preserved here.
All other secondary methods cannot surpass this supreme technique.

**The Yellow Emperor’s Nine Elixirs’ Divine Elixir Classic, Volume Seven complete.**
**Volume Eight: The Method of Transmuting *Mínghuà* Stone into Water and *Xiāoshí* (Saltpeter)**

**Preface to *Mínghuà* Stone and Water**
The humble subject has heard that in all cases of refining the Great Elixir, none can do without the power of **Mínghuà** stone and divine water.

The method of this water, though originating with the Yellow Emperor, was perfected in the Zhou dynasty through the **Bāgōng’s Thirty-Six Waters** (Eight Master’s Thirty-Six Waters).
The Eight Master refers to the teacher of the Han **Huáinán Wáng** (Addison King), **Ān Zhī** (安之).
**Liú Ān** (刘安), the Han **Huáinán Wáng** (Addison King), was the grandson of Emperor **Gāozǔ** (High Founder) and son of the **Lì Wáng** (Lethal King).
At that time, the nobles of the world all indulged in hunting and feasting with dogs and horses in the capital.
The King alone cherished the Dao of immortality, revered its sacred arts, and expounded on the Way of the Transcendent.
When he heard of those who practiced the arts of transformation and alchemy, even if they were a thousand miles away, he would send humble words and rich gifts to invite them.
They gathered like clouds, numbering thousands.

He compiled **《Nèishū》** (Inner Scriptures) in twenty-one chapters, **《Zhōngpiān》** (Middle Treatises) in eight volumes, and **《Hóngbǎo Fāng》** (Precious Treasures’ Methods) in three volumes.
He then dispatched messengers far and wide, and the Eight Master descended, moved by the King’s earnest plea to become his teacher.
Initially, the gatekeepers would not admit the Eight Master, but he presented himself in the guise of an old man and a youth.
The disciples reported this to the King, who, without stepping outside, extended his arm to summon the Master onto the **Sīxiān Zhītái** (Thought-Immortal Platform).
He set up a brocade pavilion, offered gold and jade, and took the posture of a disciple, beseeching the Master for the elixir of longevity.
The Eight Master said: *"To cultivate the Way of immortality, one must first refine the divine elixir—only then can one attain eternal life."*

*"I can transmute pastes into gold, condense mercury into silver, and with this water, transmute eight stones, causing them to fly and rise like pearls.
I can also transform the five metals and condense the seven treasures.
Those who consume it can ride the cloud dragon, float in the **Tàiqīng** (Great Clarity), enter and exit the **Zǐquè** (Purple Gate), and feast in the **Xuándū** (Mysterious Metropolis).
This is the marvel of ascending on clouds and transforming into feathers.
The King should earnestly practice it."*

**Liú Ān** (Addison King) repeatedly knelt and wept, begging for the elixir of longevity.
The Eight Master, pitying him, taught him the **《Wǔlíng Shéndān Shàngjīng》** (Five Spirits’ Divine Elixir Upper Classic) and the **Thirty-Six Waters’ Methods**.
**Liú Ān** (Addison King) then ascended the platform, swore an oath, and knelt in blood before the golden vessel to receive the divine elixir’s method.
He ignited the furnace and succeeded in refining the medicine.
Later, **Liú Ān** (Addison King) was concealed by the Five Spirits and presented to the Emperor.
The Emperor, enraged, dispatched the **Zōngzhèng** (Clan Director) with a scepter to arrest **Liú Ān** (Addison King).
The Eight Master reprimanded him: *"Alas, **Ān Zhī** (安之) can now be removed!"*
*"Those who possess the records of the immortals—if they plot, they die; if they transgress, their clans are exterminated.*
*Yet **Liú Ān** (Addison King) has not long since received them, and this is what the Eight Master warned."*

He then told **Liú Ān** (Addison King): *"Heaven has decreed such fate—how can the King not regret it?"*
The Eight Master then led **Liú Ān** (Addison King) to **Lǐngshān** (Sacred Mountain) for a grand sacrifice, and on that very day, he ascended to Heaven.
Near the great stone where he stood, the imprint of his body and horse remains visible to this day, so that even now, the rooster crows in the sky and the dog barks at the clouds.

In ancient times, the **Tàijí Zhēnrén** (Great Polarity True Person) passed down this divine method and the water-stone techniques to the **Jīnghǎi Qīngtóng Jūn** (Jade Sea Young Master).
The Young Master taught **Jīnlóu Xiānshēng** (Golden Tower Immortal Sage), who in turn taught the Eight Master.
The Eight Master then taught **Liú Ān** (Addison King), and on the day **Liú Ān** (Addison King) ascended to Heaven, he passed it to **Zuǒ Hào** (左昊).
**Zuǒ Hào** (左昧) was the one **Liú Ān** (Addison King) cherished.
As he departed, he told **Zuǒ Hào** (左昧): *"If you seek longevity, you must refine the divine elixir.
Once the divine elixir is complete, you may do as you please."*

However, these alchemical methods transmitted by the immortals are but the **Wǔlíng Shéndān** (Five Spirits’ Divine Elixir).
Though their preparation requires great caution and the selection of stones and medicines is akin to that of the Nine Elixirs, their efficacy is inferior to the **Huángdì Jiǔdǐng Shéndān** (Yellow Emperor’s Nine Elixirs’ Divine Elixir).
Yet since the **Mínghuà** stone water method originates from the Eight Master, it is thus recorded in full below.

**Method of *Huángfán* (Yellow Alum) Stone Water**
For refining the **Nine Elixirs’ Divine Elixir**, all mercury must be detoxified.
The method of detoxification cannot be achieved without **Mínghuà** stone water, for the poison will not be fully removed.
Now, the following methods all require fifty days to complete—adhering to ancient traditions, the days are not increased.

Take one *jīn* of **Mínghuà** stone (yellow alum), excluding **mǎchǐ** (horse teeth alum), and place it in a **qīngzhú tǒng** (green bamboo tube).
Thinly shave the exterior of the tube, cover it with **xiāoshí** (saltpeter) in four *liǎng* portions above and below, seal the mouth with lacquer, and immerse it in the **huápí** (flower pond).
After forty days, the water will form, which can then be mixed and applied to iron, copper, or bronze to achieve a **qīngzhú tǒng** (green bamboo tube)-like hue.

All methods employ this, and for every ten tubes, one *dǒu* (dou) of water is obtained.
Calculate the required amount of medicine accordingly, adding three *liǎng* of **shí dǎn** (stone bile).

Another method: Take three *jīn* of **Mínghuà** stone and place it in a **shēngzhú tǒng** (live bamboo tube).
Thinly shave the exterior, tightly seal the mouth, and bury it in damp soil for five days until water forms.

Yet another method: First, soak the **Mínghuà** stone in pure **chúnzhuì** (unadulterated wine) until it is thoroughly moistened.
Then place it in the tube, cover the mouth with two *liǎng* of **xiāoshí** (saltpeter) and seal it with lacquer.
Bury it three *chǐ* (feet) deep in the earth for fifteen days until water forms.

**Method of *Xuánzhū* (Black Pearl)**
Take one *jīn* of **Mínghuà** stone, three *liǎng* of **shí dǎn** (stone bile), and cover it above and below in the tube.
Seal the mouth with lacquer and immerse it in the **huápí** (flower pond) for fifty days until water forms.
All three methods employ **Xuánzhū** (black pearl) in equal measure.

**Method of the Three Essences and Six Liquids**
Take one *jīn* of **báifān shí** (white alum stone) and place it in a **qīngzhú tǒng** (green bamboo tube).
Thinly shave the exterior, cover it with two *liǎng* of **xiāoshí** (saltpeter) above and below, seal the mouth with lacquer, and immerse it in the **huápí** (flower pond) for forty days until water forms.

**Another Method for Refining the Nine Elixirs’ Divine Elixir: The Hundred Steaming and Nine Flying Method**
Take one *jīn* of **xiónghuáng** (realgar) and place it in a bamboo tube.
Add four *liǎng* of **xiāoshí** (saltpeter), seal it as per the method, and immerse it in the **huápí** (flower pond) for forty days until water forms.

**Another Method:**
Take two *liǎng* of **xiāoshí** (saltpeter) and place it in a **tángdì** (clay pot) containing bitter wine within a sealed tube. Bury it in the courtyard, three *chǐ* underground, and after twenty days, water will form—sweet in taste, yellow and turbid in color.

**Method for Refining Cinnabar Water:**
Take one *jīn* of **dānshā** (cinnabar) and place it in a fresh bamboo tube. Add two *liǎng* each of **shídǎn** (stone gall) and **xiāoshí** (saltpeter), then seal it in a **tángdì** containing bitter wine. Bury it in the courtyard, three *chǐ* underground, and after twenty days, water will form—sweet in taste, yellow and turbid in color.

**Another Method:**
Take one *jīn* of **dānshā** (cinnabar) and place it in a fresh bamboo tube. Add two *liǎng* of **shídǎn** (stone gall) and four *liǎng* of **xiāoshí** (saltpeter), seal it with lacquer as before, and immerse it in the **huápí** (flower pond) for thirty days until water forms.

**Another Method:**
Add two *liǎng* each of **shídǎn** (stone gall) and **xiāoshí** (saltpeter), seal it in a **tángdì**, and bury it as above. After thirty days, water will form—bitter in taste, red in color.

**Note by the Scholar:**
The methods for transforming **fánshí** (alum stone), **xiónghuáng** (realgar), and **dānshā** (cinnabar) into water all follow the *Bā Gōng Sānshíliù Shuǐ Zhèngjīng* (Eight Masters’ Canon of Thirty-Six Waters). All methods rely on **xiāoshí** (saltpeter) to achieve this. For transforming **dānshā** (cinnabar), **shídǎn** (stone gall) is also required.

Among the great elixirs, **róngyán** (martial salt) is essential. Though the Four Seas are now peaceful and all medicines are abundant, **xiāoshí** (saltpeter) alone remains scarce. Common folk may claim to produce water without it, or mix other substances to simulate **xiāoshí**, yet their transformations lack true efficacy. After careful investigation, I fear these methods fall short of authenticity. Thus, I record substitutes below.

**Method for Simulating Alum Stone Water with Alternative Substances:**
Take three *jīn* of **fánshí** (alum stone), grind it into powder, and place it on a mulberry-wood tray over a heated charcoal bed. After the tray has been heated overnight, sprinkle bitter wine onto the hot surface, then spread the alum powder evenly across it. Enclose the tray’s edges with white ash, wait until the heat dissipates, then remove the ash and scrape off the residue. Place it in three *dòu* of bitter wine—one *jīn* of alum to one *dòu* of wine—and steep it for seven days. When ready, boil it quickly, then let it steep for a hundred days for optimal results.

The proportions of ingredients and bitter wine may be adjusted as needed at the time of use.

**Method for Simulating Crude Saltpeter (Mángxiāoshí):**
This refers to **pǔxiāo** (crude saltpeter), not true **xiāoshí** (saltpeter). Take **pǔxiāo** and **xiāoshí**, grind them coarsely without fine sifting, and rinse the **pǔxiāo** with warm water until the liquid is clear. Boil it in any quantity, reducing it by half. Pour the concentrated solution into a small basin and add **língshuǐ** (pure water) to steep overnight. The next day, it will crystallize into angular fragments resembling white quartz. Handle it carefully to avoid contamination—if others touch it, the **qì** (vital essence) will dissipate, and the transformation will fail. Only **língshuǐ** (pure water) should be used for steeping; any impurity will hinder the process. This method ensures swift and effective transformation for all elixir waters.

**Method for Simulating Martial Salt (Róngyán) with Alternative Substances:**
Take clear **shíguàn** (stone salt) in any quantity, melt it in an iron vessel until boiling, then pour it over **báifánshí** (white alum stone) powder. Remelt and pour into brine, then remelt and pour over **gānyán** (dry salt) powder, covering it completely. Repeat this threefold refining process to produce **róngyán**. Its transformative power will match that of the genuine article.

This salt is the master of all medicines. If refining the **Wǔsè Shényán** (Five-Colored Divine Salt) or the **Wǔdì Jīng** (Essence of the Five Emperors), simply add it to achieve the desired result.

**Method for Simulating Stone Gall (Shídǎn) with Alternative Substances:**
Take two *jīn* of **qīngfánshí** (green vitriol), one *jīn* of **huángfán** (yellow vitriol), and one *jīn* of **báishānzhī** (white mountain resin). Melt them in a large iron vessel until boiling, then quickly add two *jīn* of **zhēncāngqīng** (true indigo) powder, stirring vigorously to form a paste. This yields **shídǎn**. Alternatively, if the vitriols do not fully melt, add **zhēncāngqīng** to the boiling mixture, stir with bitter water until dissolved, then pour into the vitriol mixture to form a paste, which also produces **shídǎn**.

**Method for Simulating Saltpeter (Xiāoshí) with Alternative Substances:**
Take one *jīn* each of **shípí** (stone spleen), **pǔxiāo** (crude saltpeter), and **mángxiāo** (niter). Grind each into powder, then mix with three *dòu* of bitter wine in a copper pot and boil until tenfold reduction. Strain to collect the clear liquid, then simmer gently until it thickens into a paste. Pour it into porcelain vessels and steep in **língshuǐ** (pure water) overnight. The next morning, it will crystallize like frost or ice. When water is added, it dissolves instantly. Reboil the solution, thicken it again, and return it to the porcelain vessel for another steeping in **língshuǐ**. Repeat this process, but be cautious—each refinement weakens its potency. Avoid contamination and shield it from wind and sunlight.

**Shípí** (stone spleen) is the condensed **qì** of yin and yang, the essence of the Five Salts, nurtured by alum and borne by stone. It is found in Éméi Mountain, yet common folk remain unaware of its existence. Only those who seek the Dao recognize its necessity.

The ancients, unable to find the true essence from the four directions, made substitutes that surpassed the genuine. **The method of creating a substitute for *shípí* (stone spleen):**

Take one *jīn* of true **white alum** (*zhēnbáifánshí*), one *jīn* of **military salt** (*róngyán*), and pound both finely. Then, take two *shēng* of **bitter water** (*kǔshuǐ*), place it in a copper pot, and simmer until it boils four or five times. Add the two substances, simmer until half has evaporated, then remove the dregs before boiling completely dry. The residue will rise in the pot like frost or snow—**the substitute *shípí*** (*dàiyòng shípí*), white as snow.

Use this to imitate **língshuǐ** (pure water), and it will work without fail. The marvelous and efficacious Dao is now complete.

**Master Peng** said: The true **língshuǐ** (pure water), **military salt** (*róngyán*), **stone gall** (*shípí*), and **mirabilite** (*mángxiāoshí*)—though some may possess the proper yin-yang essence—are only truly divine in their transformation and efficacy.

If one seeks immortality yet does not master this Dao, they will waste ten thousand *jīn* in vain and never succeed. The substitute *shípí* is one such substance; common folk cannot distinguish it, but its imitations all retain its nature, thus serving the purpose.

Among the **divine elixirs of longevity** (*chángshēng shényào*) compiled by **Ge Hong**, none are as precious or transformative as this method.

Now I, **Hong**, record it to show those who follow the same Way. To those unworthy—even kin—this secret is not passed for ten thousand *jīn*.

「太一曰：余闻三十六水，患在硝石。黄白之中，碍乎戎盥。
　　今具得之，道无以妄传之。后学共宝守，勿示非人。

**假别药作束野硝石法**
此药入河车，用不关丹所用也。恐合炼之际见现，俱别药成，谓便通用。今故别疏出，唯入河车。
若作河车，此硝石其药无力。今作之法，取卤咸末五斗，取三年不食井水七斗半，煮之十沸，洒取清三斗，加**芒硝**十两，煎至一斗，再以**朴硝**二斤末之，投此汁中，朴硝皆消。乃下火，澄去滓，取上用此合河车，乃诸药皆有效验耳。

**诀曰**：言束野者，束野咸味所出，河束之地也。河车法中言**晋硝**者，是此束野物也。此东野法，亦是太一於元君边，闻八种硝石法中一法也。行各偶对，不得错以河车之药，误者是**神丹**之硝石也。

**速成水法**
狐刚子注《珠经》末云：其**神泉**亦中。诸水法自有正本三十六水方，更无别作。若欲令**神泉**速成，即须着华池瓮炊宠上铁圈，承底以马通火若糠火，去瓮底七寸，置筒於瓮中，温之七十日，但令才温，好谨伺候。若作诸金石之水，尽着中同作无妨。

合此药人，不过二三，必着新净衣，不得血食在旁，即触药精，服之不得道矣。切须秘慎之。

**神泉**就变化难识，**此谓上圣之功力**。

**作三转酒法**
用赤黍米一石，酿如酒法，待熟插取，用渍够二斤，经三日许后，炊一石黍米，令至烂，更和乾够末，一时纳着瓮中，封口，**三七日**成两转。复取其牡荆根茎，合其中，微火煎之，令可五斗许，即去滓。更用七月七日**神够**末，和之一时，入瓮中，封口，还**三七日**，成也。可炼诸毒，能令刚者亦变为柔，**此名三转酒**。又**三转牡荆酒**，大丹所要能柔，又能去银毒，**与黄矾相资成因也**。好弱不毕备，金与水银等毒，**不可辄尽**。所以凡作九转九鼎大丹，必须先觅三年淳酝大醉，**其味验重，谓之左味**。投药和酿，**转作硝石华池水**。又别须作**金药华池**。**金药华池**以验五石之精，令精不扬。又须别作**三转黄白左味**，煮伏水银。仍须转此转，更投五栽华池，**若不得五栽华池**，煮伏水银，**余并非正法**。但牡刻**三转好酒**，及诸华池，**并诸石水**。有一或阙，**无以成煮炼之功也**。煮炼不了，**毒则未尽**，急事炉鼎，**其丹食毒**，不堪服也。然此其毒诸物，**不得硝石**，必无成理。

故先须得真好硝石，**与朴硝相似**，奇难别识。若得者，**必须试作雄黄、雌黄、丹砂、矾石等水者**，是硝石也。按硝石味苦辛，**实无毒**，其五脏积热，腹中止、热，止烦满，**消渴**，利小便，**久服轻身**，天地至神之石。**一名芒硝**。出益州山谷，及武都、陇西、西羌，**采无时节**。陶隐居云：主疗与朴硝相似，**经多用此硝化诸石**，竟无正别。识者顷来寻访，**犹云与朴硝同山**，所以朴硝、硝石名朴硝也。**如此则非一种也**。

先时有人得一种石，**其色理与朴硝大同小异**，脑脑如握盐雪不殊，**烧之紫青姻起**，仍成灰不停沸，**如朴硝者**，云真硝石也。**一名芒硝**。今芒硝乃是炼朴硝作之，**与皇甫说，并亦未得**。穷研其验效，**当文证记耳**。

**化硝石法三十六水方**
陇西属秦州，**在长安西羌中**，今巩昌以北，**山有咸土处**，皆有之。皇甫士安说方无朴硝，**可以硝石替之**。硝石生山之阴，**盐之胆也**。取石脾与芒硝，**以水煮之**，一斛得一二斗，**正白如雪**，以水投中，**即硝石**。」

Its taste is bitter, yet without poison. In the third month, it is gathered from the Red Mountain, and when water is applied to raw saltpeter, it dissolves immediately. Its therapeutic effects differ slightly from those of *nitre* (硝石).

According to this account, it is indeed refined *mirabilite* (芒硝) transformed into true *nitre*, but the nature of *stone spleen* (石脾) remains unclear. Since Huangfu is from the An Ding region and well-versed in medicine and herbs, he may have further clarified its refinement. Nowadays, people from Yi Province refine *alum stone* (矾石) into *nitre*, though the resulting product, though smooth and white in taste, remains fundamentally *alum stone*.

The *Kong Shi’s Prescriptions for Decoction* states: When refining *nitre*, one must boil until the liquid is entirely evaporated and ceases to bubble—yet even then, the *nitre* retains some residual liquid. The *nitre* employed by Daoist immortals must be capable of dissolving other stones, though its full potential remains unexplored.

Moreover, raw saltpeter is found in Yi Province, specifically in the mountains between the counties of Gu and Canling in Shi Province (蚕陵). It appears mostly greenish-white, sometimes with mixed irregular patches. Common folk select the white, soft portions for use as *nitre*, boiling them until the liquid bubbles forth, resembling the form of *alum stone*.

The Daoist canon repeatedly states that *nitre* can dissolve other stones. This text also asserts its ability to dissolve stone—if true, it must be so. Each should test it for themselves.

The canon further declares that raw saltpeter can refine seventy-two kinds of stone, rendering them as white as silver. When ingested, it grants lightness of body and immortality. It already encompasses eight qualities—cold, heat, astringency, slipperiness, pungency, bitterness, saltiness, and sourness—and can additionally dissolve stone. Thus, the efficacy of raw saltpeter does not differ from that of *nitre*.

The precept reads: True *nitre* is rare to obtain in good form; if not refined properly, it cannot dissolve *realgar* (雄黄) or *cinnabar* (丹砂) into liquid. If one secures the genuine substance, first extract a few *liang* (两) of *nitre* and test its ability to dissolve *realgar* and other stones. Observe whether it succeeds or fails—if it cannot dissolve stone, it is not the true substance.

Its appearance is nearly identical to raw saltpeter, yet slightly hollow and soft. To determine its authenticity, place a fragment upon burning coals: if a violet hue emerges and it then reduces to ash, it is superior. If it bubbles vigorously for an extended time, it is raw saltpeter. Given its rarity, one must also verify its efficacy by testing it with *realgar* and *cinnabar* waters—otherwise, its authenticity cannot be confirmed.

The *Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Divine Elixir Canon*, Volume Eight, concludes. The *Yellow Emperor’s Nine Cauldrons Divine Elixir Canon*, Volume Nine, concludes.

「The method of refining and ingesting gold and silver according to their purity and efficacy is as follows: Your servant has heard that gold and silver—these two treasures—can pacify the mind and internal organs. This is why the method of *Hújiāngzǐ* refining the *Xuányú* (玄珠) involves using them to bind and retain.

However, one must carefully select gold’s quality, for its efficacy cannot be overlooked. If silver is mixed with impurities, its hue will be greenish-yellow; if gold is mixed, its hue will be purplish-red. When burned, it leaves black soot upon the flesh, rendering it useless. True refined gold, by contrast, is a bright yellow-red, enduring even a hundred refinements without depletion. Though one may obtain it, it must still be hammered into thin sheets and subjected to common refining methods: salted earth is used to temper it for one day and night, then removed and repeatedly melted, hammered, and tempered until its heat is exhausted and it no longer burns. Thus, gold meant for ingestion cannot be sourced from the marketplace—it must be refined and prepared accordingly.

In ancient times, *Hújiāngzǐ* devised the *Seven Refinements* method for gold powder, all of which involved hammered gold. Now, when combined with the *Divine Elixir*, it is also proper to use hammered gold, with the freshly refined being superior. In general, when ingesting gold or silver, their toxicity must be fully eliminated before consumption is possible.
